class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.03</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-1300</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.80</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.79</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.90</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.84</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.85</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.81</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.80</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.88</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.88</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.78</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.83</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.83</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-1200H</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.77</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.79</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.78</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.80</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.75</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.81</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.80</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.85</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.77</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.79</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.80</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.81</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">In order to provide more information among different HC samples, the analysis of BET, Raman, XRD, and ICP were conducted with the results summarized in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. Their porosity characteristics were investigated by the BET measurements with both the CO<sub>2</sub> and the N<sub>2</sub> gases, which differentiates the ultra micropores and the micro/mesopores, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> It is revealed that the specific surface area has decreased from 49.7 to 42.9, 29.7, 11.3, and 11.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> by the increase of temperature from 800 to 1300 °C. The reduced specific surface area has been highlighted to limit the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and therefore benefits to achieve higher ICE. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 41 to 44">(41−44)</a> The calcination temperature is found to determine the pore size distributions significantly. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> As suggested by <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, there are generally micro- and mesopores in HC materials with a pore diameter of 1–12 nm. Accordingly, mesopores (2–12 nm) are mainly suggested in HC-800, HC-1000, and HC-1100, whereas these mesopores are greatly reduced when the calcination temperature increases. These pores are generated due to the decomposition of organic precursors and the unique porous hierarchy of plant precursors, which promotes the Na-ion diffusion/adsorption and contributes to the increase of capacity utilization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> However, the ordering of graphite crystallite (graphitization process) is enhanced by the high temperature during the pyrolysis, which attributes to the decrease of mesopores occurred at 1200 °C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Meanwhile, the specific surface area of HC increases from 11.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> (HC-1200) to 12.7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> (HC-1200H) before and after the acid-washing. Such a phenomenon is resulted from the removal of heterogeneous components, where the acids lead to the destruction of HC, and thereafter propagate cracks to open these micropores. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/medium/am9b22745_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Physical properties of the prepared HC materials: (a) pore size distributions of HC determined by NLDFT calculation of the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption measurements (the inset shows the CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption measurements); (b) Raman spectra of the HC; (c) XRD patterns of HC prepared at different calcination temperature; (d) XRD patterns of HC prepared at 1200 °C before and after the acid-washing; (e) content analysis of ICP on the major elements of HC-1200 and HC-1200H.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22745&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Raman analysis demonstrates the change of the graphitization during the calcination process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). Two peaks are observed at around 1339 and 1589 cm<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to the disordered structure or defects (D-band) and the ordered graphitic structure (G-band), respectively. The graphitization of HC materials was quantitatively measured by the intensity ratio of these two bands (<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> In this case, the <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> ratio is calculated to be 0.61, 0.69, 0.75, 0.78, and 0.82 for the HC-800, HC-1000, HC-1100, HC-1200, and HC-1300, respectively (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>). As is expected, the high temperature promotes the graphitization and reduces defects in lattice. It is noteworthy that the <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> ratio of HC-1200H is decreased to be 0.75. The difference of <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> ratios between HC-1200 and HC-1200H can be interpreted as the destruction and the removal of Ca ions in microstructure during the process of acid-washing. In addition, the graphitization facilitates the electronic conductivity of HC materials, which is an advantage to improve their rate performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">XRD analysis was further conducted to confirm the changes of the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing of HC materials due to the calcination and Ca ion self-incorporation. All of the XRD spectra shows two characteristic peaks at 22 and 43° corresponding to the (002) and (101) plane. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref24 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref24 ref50" aria-label="reference 3, 24 and 50">(3,24,50)</a> The interlayer distance based on XRD patterns are calculated and summarized in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, according to the Bragg equation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>) as following<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/medium/am9b22745_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>d</i> is the average interlayer spacing, θ is the diffraction angle, λ is the wavelength of the incident X-ray excitation beam, <i>n</i> is the order of reflection. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Accordingly, the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing of HC materials decreases from 0.4205 to 0.4163, 0.3954, 0.4028, and 0.3829 nm with the increase of the calcination temperature. Again, the acid-washing is proved to trigger the decrease of the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing of HC materials from 0.4028 to 0.3812 nm for the HC-1200 and HC-1200H, respectively. This phenomenon clearly highlights that the Ca ions between interlayers engage to expand the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing during the calcination process due to the hyperaccumulation effect and the characteristics of being well distributed. In addition, peaks at 16.9, 24.1, 28.4, and 34.3° are observed with the unwashed HC materials, which can be indexed to the (101), (121), (031), and (040) planes of the KCaCl<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS card no. 21-1170), whereas no XRD peaks of the KCaCl<sub>3</sub> are found after acid-washing. The KCaCl<sub>3</sub> (chlorocalcite) are identified as mineral inclusions in halite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> On account of the hyperaccumulation of plants, Ca ions, K ions, and Cl ions are selectively absorbed and enriched in the halophytic plants and widely dispersed in tissues. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> It is suggested that despite of the very limited amount of KCaCl<sub>3</sub> crystals, the majority of adsorbed Ca ions are evenly distributed into the interlayers. In addition, the ICP-OES tests were conducted to demonstrate the Ca-rich characteristics of HC materials. The content of Ca reduces from ∼2.43 (wt) % to less than 0.5(wt) % by the acid-washing process. The results also reveal that the content of Ca ions is several orders of magnitude higher than that of the other components, such as K ions, proving that the hyperaccumulation and the incorporation of Ca ions constitutes the major contribution to the lattice difference in HC materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref52" aria-label="reference 26, 27 and 52">(26,27,52)</a> Notably, the concentrations of Ca and other heterogeneous components reduce drastically after acid-washing. In addition, the main discrepancy in electrochemical performance between HC-1200 and HC-1200H could be attributed to the lattice changes caused by the removal of heterogeneous components.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to further confirm the elemental composition of HC materials, XPS of C 1s and Ca 2p between HC-1200 and HC-1200H were compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. In terms of C 1s spectra, three typical peaks can be deconvoluted with the HC-1200, including the 284.7 eV (C–C, 70.15%), 285.6 eV (C–O, 10.04%), and 287.8 eV (O–C═O, 2.77%). On the other hand, the HC-1200H gives rise to peaks of the similar binding energy with different ratios, including the 284.8 eV (C–C, 67.59%), 285.6 eV (C–O, 25.21%), and 287.8 eV (O–C═O, 7.20%). It is worth noting that an additional peak at 287.0 eV can be identified with HC-1200 corresponding to the C–Cl bond. Both the KCl and CaCl<sub>2</sub> are reported to bound to the defects of HC materials through the C–Cl bond. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36 ref53" aria-label="reference 35, 36 and 53">(35,36,53)</a> Also, the C–Cl bond can reside in the interlayer spacing or edges of carbon layers to balance the charges of the excess cation, which is favorable for the stability of residue Ca ions in lattice. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref54" aria-label="reference 49 and 54">(49,54)</a> In this regard, the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms are arranged in conjugated honeycomb lattices, relating to the graphitized carbon, while the sp<sup>3</sup> carbon atoms refer to the defects on graphite layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref55" aria-label="reference 9 and 55">(9,55)</a> By contrast, the C 1s spectra shows higher content of sp<sup>3</sup> carbon and O–C═O bonds after acid-washing, whereas the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms greatly decrease. It stands to the reason that more defects on graphite structures have been created due to the removal of Ca ions in lattice. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref56" aria-label="reference 27 and 56">(27,56)</a> In addition, the content of Ca ions is indeed reduced by the comparison of Ca 1s spectra before and after the acid-washing. Based on the analysis aforementioned, the expansion of the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing of HC materials has been proposed by natural Ca ions through a natural hyperaccumulative process, which can regulate the lattice structure as well as their characteristics of Na-ion storage and diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/medium/am9b22745_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. XPS analysis between HC-1200 and HC-1200H: C 1s XPS spectra of the (a) HC-1200 and (b) HC-1200H samples; and the Ca 2p XPS spectra of the (c) HC-1200 and (d) HC-1200H samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22745&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.1.  Electrochemical Properties</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to elucidate the effect of the natural Ca-incorporation on electrochemical properties of HC materials, the redox behaviors are compared between HC-1200 and HC-1200H through the CV and charge/discharge profiles in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. The CV curves of the initial several cycles are displayed between 0.01 and 2.50 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,d. Two cathodic peaks located at ∼0.4 and ∼1.1 V can be observed at the initial cycle, which disappear thereafter. It corresponds to an irreversible SEI formation, which could unavoidably lead to the decrease of ICE. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 58 to 60">(58−60)</a> On the contrary, a pair of peaks at ∼0.1 V maintains reversibly, which results from the intercalation/extraction of Na ions into/from HC materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 28, 61 and 62">(28,61,62)</a> The consequent CV cycles exhibit very similar redox curves, indicating the high reversibility and stability of the electrode reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60 ref63 ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 60, 63 and 64">(60,63,64)</a> As compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b,e, the HC-1200 shows a reversible capacity of 326.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with the ICE of 70.39%, whereas the HC-1200H shows a lower discharge capacity of 311.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with an ICE of 68.96%. It confirms that the removal of Ca ions in lattice has showed the negative effect to their capacity utilization probably due to the narrowed <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing and the defects, as suggested by the BET, TEM, and XRD results aforementioned. Furthermore, the discharge/charge profiles contain a plateau region (generally &lt;0.1 V), derived from the Na-ion insertion into carbon layers, and a sloping region from the Na-ion absorption at surface or defects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref60 ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref60 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 28 and 60 to 62">(28,60−62)</a> As summarized and compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,f, both the HC-1200 and the HC-1200H show a decrease in the slope capacity due to the formation of SEI at the initial three cycles. The proportion of the platform capacity for the HC-1200 is larger than that of HC-1200H, which also indicates that the acid-washing has changed the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/medium/am9b22745_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical redox behavior of the prepared HC-1200 and HC-1200H: the CV curves (a,d), the charge/discharge profiles (b,e) and the capacity of sloping/plateau for the initial three cycles (c,f) for the HC-1200 and HC-1200H, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22745&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cycling performance of HC samples was compared with respect of the capacity ratio of the plateau and slope, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a presents the long cycling performance at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The HC-1200 sample provides the highest capacity among these HC samples. Specifically, the HC-1200 shows no distinct capacity fading over 250 cycles and delivers a reversible capacity of 326.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with excellent cycle stability. In addition, the specific capacities of HC-800, HC-1000, HC-1100, HC-1300, and HC-1200H reach to 196.8, 250.6, 282.1, 312.1, and 311.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 250 cycles, respectively. Furthermore, as compared by <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b,c, the capacity proportion from the slope region is identified to be 69.9, 63.6, 58.5, 45.1, and 43.6% for the sample of HC-800, HC-1000, HC-1100, HC-1200, and HC-1300, respectively. A decrease in the capacity proportion from the slope region can be observed due to the graphitization by raising the temperature. As the calcination temperature increases, the nanopores and defects are decreasing and the local ordering of carbon layers continues to increase. It is beneficial to improve the ICE as well as the electrochemical performance. The ICEs are 50.35, 61.48, 67.45, and 70.39% with the sample of HC-800, HC-1000, HC-1100, and HC-1200, and it is apparently promoted by the pyrolysis at a higher temperature. The low-graphitized HC material (HC-800 e.g.) delivers a very limited plateau capacity (around ∼58.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and the 30.1% proportion of plateau capacity), which is the main reason to the insufficient capacity utilization. On the other hand, highly graphitized HC materials (e.g., HC-1300) with the reduced <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing seem to be not appropriate for capacity utilization because the HC-1300 (∼174.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) offers less capacity than that of HC-1200 (∼180.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). It highly suggests that the narrowed <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing will hinder the ionic diffusion in lattice due to the relatively large ionic radius of Na ions. Therefore, the pyrolysis temperature plays an important role to balance the graphitization, porosity, and the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing and thus optimize the specific capacity obtained from both the plateau and the slope. As additional bonus, the ICEs are improved with the sample pyrolyzed at a higher temperature. The change of ICEs are due to the change of microstructures and porosity with materials pyrolyzed at different temperatures. Higher pyrolysis temperature promotes the degree of graphitization and reduces the defect sites on the surface of the material, the pore structure is more conducive to the storage of Na-ion, but the ICEs will decrease slightly when the temperature continues to increase. The Coulomb efficiency of HC-1300 is 67.52%. This is because when the temperature is further increased, the interlayer distance becomes smaller, and some active sites become inactive due to the formation of irreversible sites and the narrowed <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing, resulting in the reduced ICEs.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/medium/am9b22745_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical properties of the prepared HC materials: (a) cycling performance at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with different samples; (b) charge/discharge profiles of HC with different samples; (C) capacity of sloping/plateau of HC with different samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22745&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate performance of different samples was further compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>. As is expected, the capacity of HC decreases with the increase of the current density (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a). The HC-1200 sample shows the optimal rate performance, delivering the capacity of 323.9, 270.7, 198.6, 93.6, 43.9, and 24 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The capacity can be restored to 317.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> as the current density goes back to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Meanwhile, the HC-1300 delivers the capacity of 311.2, 214.4, 163.5, 65.4, 25.0, and 15.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> from 50 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> due to the enhanced graphitization. However, the HC-800 delivers the capacity of 171.9, 148.2, 118.1, 67.5, 44.0, and 32.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, and the HC-1000 delivers the capacity of 243.9, 218.3, 162.7, 77.3, 42.9, and 28.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, whereas the HC-1100 delivers the capacity of 267.9, 228.7, 184.0, 86.0, 42.9 and 28.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. All these three samples deliver limited capacity utilization compared to that of the HC-1200 and HC-1300. In addition, the reduction of the platform capacity is considered as the main reason to the low capacity utilization (as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b). It can be concluded from the rate performance that the graphitization and the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing can also determine the ion mobility and capacity retention during rate tests and thus gives rise to the different rate capability by the samples obtained at different temperatures. High graphitization gives rise to relatively high electronic conductivity to boost the rate capability, whereas the associated narrowed <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing and the loss of micropores constitute the major obstacle for the ion mobility. As a good balance between the graphitization and the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing, the HC-1200 delivers the optimized rate performance.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/medium/am9b22745_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Rate capability and EIS properties of prepared HC materials: (a) rate performance of HC with different samples; (b) percentages of capacity of sloping/plateau at various current densities; (c) EIS analyses of HC prepared at different calcination temperature with equivalent circuits inset; (d) relationship between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> of HC prepared at different calcination temperature in the low frequency; (e) EIS analyses of HC prepared with/without acid-washing (equivalent circuits inset); (f) the relationship between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> of HC-1200 and HC-1200H in the low frequency.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/large/am9b22745_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22745&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">In order to figure out the electrochemical kinetics of HC materials, the EIS measurements were carried out and the corresponding Nyquist plots are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c,e. The semicircle in high-frequency region is attributed to the resistance from the charge transfer (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), whereas the linear region corresponds to the Warburg diffusion of Na ions within HC materials. Accordingly, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> are calculated to be 1391, 1146, 805, 568, 489, and 594 Ω for the HC-800, HC-1000, HC-1100, HC-1200, HC-1300, and HC-1200H, respectively. Significantly, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the HC-1200 is the lowest among all the samples, which is ascribed to the balance between the higher degree of graphitization (high electronic conductivity) and the optimized <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing. In addition, the Na-ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) can be calculated from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eqs 2">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a> as following<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/medium/am9b22745_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22745/asset/images/medium/am9b22745_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i13"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>where <i>R</i> is the ideal gas constant, <i>T</i> is the Kelvin temperature in the experiment, <i>A</i> is the area of working electrode, <i>N</i> is the number of electrons transferred during the chemical reaction, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant, <i>C</i> is the Na-ion (4.9 × 10<sup>–4</sup> mol cm<sup>–3</sup>) concentration in the electrode, <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> is the resistance of the electrolyte, <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> is the real part of resistance, and σ is the Warburg factor which is related to <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d displays the correlation between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> at low frequencies. As a result, the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> is calculated to be 3.80 × 10<sup>–14</sup>, 1.57 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, 2.43 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, 3.68 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, 1.75 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, and 1.75 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for the HC-800, HC-1000, HC-1100, HC-1200, HC-1300, and HC-1200H, respectively. Again, the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> is also found to be determined by the balance between the graphitization and <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing caused by the calcination process. Therefore, the optimization on the calcination temperature proves to be important on regulating the lattice structure of HC materials as well as their SIB performance, which is facile and cost-effective for their large-scale application.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A promising natural Ca ion self-incorporated HC was derived from tamarind fruits as the SIB anode via the pyrolysis method. This work clearly highlighted that the regulation on the calcination temperature was significant for the porosity, lattice structure, and SIB performance. HC materials prepared at 1200 °C demonstrated the best overall performances due to the appropriate nanopores, defects, electronic conductivity, and the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing, optimal for the Na-ion storage. Significantly, the natural enriched Ca ions via the hyperaccumulation process proved to further expand the <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing, which fulfilled the cation self-incorporation with good distribution for the ion diffusion. Optimized <i>d</i>-interlayer spacing was more conducive to the Na-ion intercalation/extraction during the charge/discharge process, which leaded to the increase of the platform capacity and promoted the electrochemical performance of HC materials. As a result, the HC anode exhibited excellent electrochemical performances, delivering the reversible capacity of 326.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and capacity retention rate of 89.40% after 250 cycles with an ICE of 70.39%. This work provides an insightful guidance on the utilization of self-incorporated HC materials for the large-scale application of energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="_suppInfo"></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b22745" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="authorInformationSection"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="authorInformationSection">Author Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><ul><li><span class="author-information-subsection-header">Corresponding Authors</span><ul><li><div class="authorItemInformation"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor strong">Xinran Wang</span> - <span class="hlFld-Affiliation affiliation">Beijing Key Laboratory

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ceder, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">710</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">721</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201200026</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D710-721%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DS.-W.%2BKim%26author%3DD.-H.%2BSeo%26author%3DX.%2BMa%26author%3DG.%2BCeder%26author%3DK.%2BKang%26title%3DElectrode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BRechargeable%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BPotential%2BAlternatives%2Bto%2BCurrent%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201200026&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b22745&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201200026&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c7ta00639j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Kelp-derived Hard Carbons as Advanced Anode Materials for Sodium-ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5761</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5769</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c7ta00639j</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D5761-5769%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DP.%2BWang%26author%3DX.%2BZhu%26author%3DQ.%2BWang%26author%3DX.%2BXu%26author%3DX.%2BZhou%26author%3DJ.%2BBao%26title%3DKelp-derived%2BHard%2BCarbons%2Bas%2BAdvanced%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc7ta00639j&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b22745&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc7ta00639j&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Kelp-derived hard carbons as advanced anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Pengzi; Zhu, Xiaoshu; Wang, Qiaoqiao; Xu, Xin; Zhou, Xiaosi; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5761-5769</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received much attention for scalable elec. energy storage because of the abundance and wide availability of sodium resources.  However, it is still unclear whether carbon anodes can realize large-scale com. application in SIBs as in lithium-ion batteries.  Recently, great attention has been devoted to hard carbon which has been treated as a promising choice.  Herein, we observe that the turbostratic lattice of kelp-derived hard carbon (KHC) is repeatedly expandable and shrinkable upon cycling, where the interlayer distance varies between 3.9 and 4.3 Å.  Such interlayer spacing dilation is highly reversible, giving rise to high rate capability (a stable capacity of 96 mA h g-1 at 1000 mA g-1) and excellent cycling performance (205 mA h g-1 after 300 cycles at 200 mA g-1).  Furthermore, kelp-derived hard carbon exhibits a good specific capacity at potentials higher than 0.05 V, which make it an essentially dendrite-free anode for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2019.09.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sean, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Superior sodium-storage behavior of flexible anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> promoted by oxygen vacancies</span>. <i>Energy Storage Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>25</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">903</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">911</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Inhibition of Crystallization of Poly(ethylene oxide) by Ionic Liquid: Insight into Plasticizing Mechanism and Application for Solid-State Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Guanghai; Bai, Ying; Gao, Yongsheng; Wang, Zhaohua; Zhang, Kun; Ni, Qiao; Wu, Feng; Xu, Huajie; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">All-solid-state sodium ion batteries (ASIBs) possess enhanced safety and desired cycling life compared with conventional liq. sodium batteries, showing great potential in large-scale energy storage systems.  Polymer electrolytes based on poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) have been extensively studied for ASIBs due to superior flexibility and processability.  However, PEO-based electrolyte without any modification can hardly meet the requirements of ASIBs at room temp.  In the past decade, unremitting efforts have been attached to inhibiting crystn. of PEO, esp. via ionic liq. plasticizing.  However, the plasticizing mechanism is not clear.  Here the Pyr13FSI is incorporated into PEO-NaClO4 electrolyte to investigate the plasticizing effect by IR spectrum characterizations and DFT calcns.  The results indicate that FSI- anions tend to adhere to the PEO backbone, generating enhanced coordination ability and more coordination sites.  Solid-state sodium ion batteries using PEO-NaClO4-40 wt. % Pyr13FSI as polymer electrolyte exhibit good cycling and rate performance.  Insights into the plasticizing mechanism contribute to fabricating polymer electrolyte with high performance for ASIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/c7ta02515g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.-E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Boosting the Rate Capability of Hard Carbon with an Ether-based Electrolyte for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Capacity Interstitial Mn-Incorporated MnxFe3-xO4/Graphene Nanocomposite for Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ren, Haixia; Bai, Ying; Wang, Xinran; Ni, Qiao; Wang, Zhaohua; Li, Yu; Chen, Guanghai; Wu, Feng; Xu, Huajie; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted wide attention because of their prospects for grid-scale elec. regulation and cost effectiveness of Na.  In this regard, FeOx are considered as one of the most promising anode candidates due to their high theor. capacity and low cost.  Unfortunately, the utilization of FeOx anodes suffers from sluggish reaction kinetics and significant lattice variation, causing insufficient rate performance and fast capacity degrdn. during the sodiation/desodiation process.  Here, Mn ions are incorporated through interstitial sites into a Fe3O4 lattice to form the Mn-incorporated Fe3O4/graphene (M-Fe3O4/G) composites through a facile hydrothermal method.  Confirmed by XRD Rietveld refinement and the first-principles calcn., Mn occupation into the body structure can effectively condense the electron d. around the Fermi level and thus contributes to the increased elec. cond. and improved electrochem. properties.  Accordingly, the M0.1Fe2.9O4/G composite demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 439.8 mAh/g at a c.d. of 100 mA/g over 200 cycles.  Even at a high c.d. of 1 A/g, the M-Fe3O4/G composites remain stable for over 1200 cycles, delivering a capacity of 210 mAh/g.  Coupled with a Na3V2(PO4)3-type cathode, the Mn-incorporated Fe3O4/G composites demonstrate good suitability in full SIBs (161.2 mAh/g at the c.d. of 1 A/g after 100 cycles).                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Probing the Failure Mechanism of SnO2 Nanowires for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Meng; Kushima, Akihiro; Shao, Yuyan; Zhang, Ji-Guang; Liu, Jun; Browning, Nigel D.; Li, Ju; Wang, Chongmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5203-5211</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Non-lithium metals such as Na have attracted attention as a potential charge carrying ion for rechargeable batteries.  Using in situ TEM in combination with d. functional theory calcns., the authors probed the structural and chem. evolution of SnO2 nanowire anodes in Na-ion batteries and compared them quant. with results from Li-ion batteries.  Upon Na insertion into SnO2, a displacement reaction occurs, giving amorphous NaxSn nanoparticles dispersed in Na2O matrix.  With further Na insertion, the NaxSn crystd. into Na15Sn4 (x = 3.75).  Upon extn. of Na (de-sodiation), the NaxSn transforms to Sn nanoparticles.  Assocd. with the de-alloying, pores form, leading to a structure of Sn particles confined in a hollow matrix of Na2O.  These pores greatly increase elec. impedance, therefore accounting for the poor cyclability of SnO2.  DFT calcns. indicate that Na+ diffuses 30 times slower than Li+ in SnO2, in agreement with in situ TEM measurement.  Insertion of Na can chemomechanically soften the reaction product to a greater extent than in lithiation.  Therefore, in contrast to the lithiation of SnO2 significantly less dislocation plasticity was seen ahead of the sodiation front.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Evolution of disposable bamboo chopsticks into uniform carbon fibers: a smart strategy to fabricate sustainable anodes for Li-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang, Jian; Zhu, Jianhui; Ai, Wei; Fan, Zhanxi; Shen, Xiaonan; Zou, Chenji; Liu, Jinping; Zhang, Hua; Yu, Ting</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Future development of mini consumer electronics or large elec. vehicles/power grids requires Li-ion batteries (LIBs) with not only an outstanding energy-storage performance but also a min. cost, and the foremost sustainability.  Herein, we put forward a smart strategy to convert used disposable bamboo chopsticks into uniform carbon fibers for anodes of LIBs.  Bamboo chopsticks waste is recycled and simply treated by a controllable hydrothermal process performed in alk. solns., wherein abundant natural cellulose fibers in bamboo in situ get sepd. and dispersed spontaneously.  After carbonization, the evolved carbon fibers exhibit superior anodic performance to the bulky bamboo carbons counterpart, and competitive electrochem. behavior and cost with com. graphite. A high reversible capacity of ∼710 mA h g-1 is maintained without decay up to 300 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A porous hard carbon material was synthesized by the simple pyrolysis of H3PO4-treated biomass, i.e., pomelo peels, at 700 °C in N2.  The as-obtained hard carbon had a 3D connected porous structure and a large sp. surface area of 1272 m2 g-1.  XPS anal. showed that the carbon material was functionalized by O-contg. and P-contg. groups.  The porous hard carbon was used as an anode for sodium ion batteries and exhibited good cycling stability and rate capability, delivering a capacity of 181 mA h g-1 at 200 mA g-1 after 220 cycles and retaining a capacity of 71 mA h g-1 at 5 A g-1.  The sodium storage mechanisms of the porous hard carbon can be explained by Na+ intercalation into the disordered graphene layers, redox reaction of the surface O-contg. functional groups and Na+ storage in the nanoscale pores.  However, the porous hard carbon demonstrated a low coulombic efficiency of 27%, resulting from the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase film and the side reactions of surface phosphorus groups.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1007/s10008-016-3485-z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Sustainable Synthesis of Biomass Carbon Sheets as Excellent Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K+ Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Feng; Liu, Lu; Yuan, Yifei; Li, Yu; Bai, Ying; Li, Tao; Lu, Jun; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Heteroatom-doped carbon is an attractive material for anodes in lithium-/sodium-ion batteries as a replacement for traditional graphite anodes.  However, the complex fabrication process and high cost limit practical applications of these carbon materials.  Here, a low-cost, natural potassium-doped carbon material is reported which is directly carbonized from the coconut endocarp, a kind of high potassium-contg. biomass material.  The obtained carbon structure features an expanded d(002)-spacing (0.4 nm) originating from the superhigh potassium content (6654 mg kg-1).  Because of the improvement on charge transfer kinetics and elec. properties, the potassium-doped carbon anode exhibits promising electrochem. performance in sodium-ion batteries, including high initial reversible capacity (314 mAh g-1) and good cycle stability (289 mAh g-1 after 200 cycles).  Addnl., this work opens up a new approach for the design of heteroatom-doped carbon materials from the viewpoint of being naturally environmental friendly.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sushko, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Correlating compn. and structures with battery performance is key aspect of electrode material design and improvement.  Here utilizing in situ open cell transmission electron microscopy, we studied the in situ cycling rate performance of vanadium oxide coated tin dioxide nanowire electrode by tuning the lithiation/delithiation current.  In situ results show that the good rate performance of such high capacity compositional material lies in the layered vanadium oxide coating strategy.  For cycling at high rate, the layered vanadium oxide also serves as fast ions and electrons transportation route while tin nanoparticles aggregate to the surface with sizes controlled by the coating layer, cycle induced vol. change is released to the surface and excellent mech. tolerance of tin nanoparticle and inner nanowire ensure improved cyclability of the electrode.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms13432"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mei, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shakir, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duan, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Hyperaccumulation Pathway to Three-dimensional Hierarchical Porous Nanocomposites for Highly Robust High-power Electrodes</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A hyperaccumulation pathway to three-dimensional hierarchical porous nanocomposites for highly robust high-power electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu Jian; Wang Tao; Mei Lin; Xu Zhi; Lu Bingan; Zhu Jian; Sun Hongtao; Mei Lin; Duan Xiangfeng; Shan Yu; Zhao Zipeng; Fan Zheng; Huang Yu; Shakir Imran; Huang Yu; Duan Xiangfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">13432</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Natural plants consist of a hierarchical architecture featuring an intricate network of highly interconnected struts and channels that not only ensure extraordinary structural stability, but also allow efficient transport of nutrients and electrolytes throughout the entire plants.  Here we show that a hyperaccumulation effect can allow efficient enrichment of selected metal ions (for example, Sn(2+), Mn(2+)) in the halophytic plants, which can then be converted into three-dimensional carbon/metal oxide (3DC/MOx) nanocomposites with both the composition and structure hierarchy.  The nanocomposites retain the 3D hierarchical porous network structure, with ultrafine MOx nanoparticles uniformly distributed in multi-layers of carbon derived from the cell wall, cytomembrane and tonoplast.  It can simultaneously ensure efficient electron and ion transport and help withstand the mechanical stress during the repeated electrochemical cycles, enabling the active material to combine high specific capacities typical of batteries and the cycling stability of supercapacitors.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1016/j.jep.2015.10.031"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Van
Wyk, B.-E.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Review of Commercially Important African Medicinal Plants</span>. <i>J. Ethnopharmacol.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>176</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">118</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">134</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jep.2015.10.031</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D176%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D118-134%26journal%3DJ.%2BEthnopharmacol.%26author%3DB.-E.%2BVan%250AWyk%26title%3DA%2BReview%2Bof%2BCommercially%2BImportant%2BAfrican%2BMedicinal%2BPlants%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jep.2015.10.031&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b22745&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jep.2015.10.031&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review of commercially important African medicinal plants</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Van Wyk B-E</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of ethnopharmacology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">176</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">118-34</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">ETHNOPHARMACOLOGICAL RELEVANCE:  Data on the relative importance and research status of commercially relevant African medicinal plants are needed for developing new research strategies in order to stimulate much-needed ethnopharmacological research and to promote the commercialization of African plants.  AIM OF THE STUDY:  To present an illustrated bird's eye view and comparative analysis of the relative popularity and importance of commercialized African medicinal plants.  A comparison is made between the general popularity and commercial importance of the species (as indicated by their footprint on the World Wide Web) and their scientific popularity and importance (as indicated by the number of research publications).  The inventory and review is strongly focussed to cover all or most of the medicinal plant raw materials in the international trade that are exported from African countries, with less emphasis on those that are regularly traded on local and regional markets within Africa.  MATERIALS AND METHODS:  The review is based on literature data, Scopus and Google searches, commercial information and the author's own experience and observations.  RESULTS:  More than 5400 plant species are used in traditional medicine in Africa, of which less than 10% have been commercially developed to some extent.  Africa is home to more than 80 valuable commercial species that are regularly traded on international markets, including phytomedicines (e.g.  Harpagophytum procumbens and Pelargonium sidoides), functional foods (e.g.  Adansonia digitata and Hibiscus sabdariffa) and sources of pure chemical entities (e.g. caffeine from Coffea arabica and yohimbine from Pausinystalia johimbe).  According to the Scopus results, about 60% of all recent publications on African medicinal plants appeared in the last decade, with an average of 280 papers (28 per year) for 85 prominent species of international trade.  The most popular African species for research (number of publications in brackets) were: Ricinus communis (5187), Aloe vera (2832), Catharanthus roseus (2653), Sesamum indicum (2534), Strophanthus gratus (2514), Coffea arabica (2431), Citrullus lanatus (2215), Momordica charantia (2047), Withania somnifera (1767), Trigonella foenum-graecum (1687), Acacia senegal (1373), Centella asiatica (1355), Griffonia simplicifolia (1010), Hibiscus sabdariffa (987), Tamarindus indica (973) and Catha edulis (947).  The top species in terms of recent research interest (% of publications in last decade) were: Hoodia gordonii (95%), Cyclopia genistoides (93%), Sceletium tortuosum (90%), Agathosma betulina (89%), Pelargonium sidoides (86%), Boswellia papyrifera (85%), Lessertia (Sutherlandia) frutescens (84%), Boswellia sacra (83%), Mondia whitei (81%), Hibiscus sabdariffa (80%), Hypoxis hemerocallidea (80%) and Tylosema esculentum (80%).  Both lists reflect the recent interest in functional foods and dietary supplements.  CONCLUSION:  Despite a marked recent increase in the number of publications on indigenous medicinal plants and dietary supplements, Africa lags behind Europe and Asia in terms of the number of products that have been commercialised and the percentage of the flora that is utilized for international trade.  There is a tremendous potential for developing new crops and new products but much work remains to be done to generate more focussed and relevant pre-clinical data and convincing proof of concept through clinical studies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1007/s10800-016-0974-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ramamoorthy, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Radha, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maheswari, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Anandan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manoharan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Victor Williams, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Betalain and Anthocyanin Dye-sensitized Solar Cells</span>. <i>J. It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1016/j.enconman.2017.06.022"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Omoriyekomwan, J. Two of them, CaC6 and Li3Ca2C6, exhibit superconducting properties at 11.5 K and 11.15 K, resp., the highest crit. temps. among those of graphite intercalation compds.  The samples are synthesized using a liq.-solid method allowing the prepn. of pure bulk samples, auspicious for crystallog. and magnetic measurements.  The crystal structure of CaC6 was entirely specified; this compd. crystallizes in the R-3m space group. In both cases the magnetic phase diagram indicates that these compds. are type II superconducting materials slightly anisotropic in spite of their lamellar structure.  In the case of CaC6, in-plane magnetic penetration depth measurements show a clear exponential behavior at low temps., consistent with an s-wave symmetry of the gap function, well fitted by the std.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Methyl Radical Reactivity on the Basal Plane of Graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mandeltort, Lynn; Choudhury, Pabitra; Johnson, J. Karl; Yates, John T.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The reaction of submonolayer Li atoms with MeCl at 100 K on a highly oriented pyrolytic graphite (HOPG) surface was studied under ultrahigh vacuum.  The authors exploit the low defect d. of the high quality HOPG used here (∼109 defects cm-2) to eliminate the effects of step edges and defects on the graphite surface chem.  Li causes C-Cl bond scission in MeCl, liberating Me radicals &lt;130 K.  Ordinarily, 2 Me species would couple to form products such as C2H6, but in the presence of graphite, Me preferentially adsorbs on the flat basal plane of Li-treated graphite.  A C-Me bond of 1.2 eV is formed, which is enhanced relative to Me binding to clean graphite (0.52 eV) due to donation of electrons from Li into the graphite and back-donation from graphite to Me.  A low yield of C1, C2, and C3 hydrocarbon products &gt;330 K is found along with a low yield of H2.  The low yield of these products indicates that the majority of the Me groups are irreversibly bound to the basal plane of graphite, and only a small fraction participate in the prodn. of C1-C3 volatile products or in extensive dehydrogenation.  Spin-polarized d. functional theory calcns. indicate that Me binds to the Li-treated surface with an activation energy of 0.3 eV to form a C-Me adsorbed surface species with sp3 hybridization of the graphite, and the Me C atoms is involved in bond formation.  Bound Me radicals become mobile with 0.7 eV activation energy and can participate in combination reactions for the prodn. of small yields of C1-C3 hydrocarbon products.  Alkyl radical attachment to the graphite surface is kinetically preferred over hydrocarbon product desorption.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms14283"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.-Y.</span></span>;                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extended flat voltage profile of hard carbon synthesized using a two-step carbonization approach as an anode in sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alvin, Stevanus; Yoon, Dohyeon; Chandra, Christian; Susanti, Ratna F.; Chang, Wonyoung; Ryu, Changkook; Kim, Jaehoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is a promising anode material for sodium ion batteries (NIBs).  In this study, a two-step carbonization approach is developed to enhance the electrochem. performance of lignocellulose biomass-derived hard carbon.  The first step comprises slow low-temp. pyrolysis of fir wood that produces an amorphous carbon in which hexagonal planes are embedded in the amorphous carbon region to some extent.  The second step comprises high-temp. carbonization at 1300 °C, which yields a hard carbon with a high degree of graphitization, an increased layer-plane length, and a low micropore vol.  Two-step carbonized hard carbon delivers a large reversible capacity of 276 mAh g-1 at 50 mA g-1 after 100 cycles and high rate capacities of 108 mAh g-1 at 1.0 A g-1 and 76.3 mAh g-1 at 2.5 A g-1.  The low-voltage plateau capacity below 0.1 V is 194 mAh g-1.  The results of these expts. indicate that the exceptional electrochem. performance of two-step carbonized hard carbon arises from the effective suppression of micropore formation and a good balance between the degree of graphitization and no. of defect sites.  High-voltage adsorption of Na+ ions in micropores inhibits Na+-ion diffusion into the graphitic region of micropore-enriched hard carbon.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref39" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit39" data-doi="10.1021/ja802474b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Culp, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rare hysteretic adsorption/desorption isotherms are reported for CO2 and N2 on a pillared Ni(1,2-bis(4-pyridyl)ethylene)[Ni(CN)4] compd. (NiBpeneNiCN).  The hysteresis occurs under moderate pressure and at temps. above the crit. temps. of the resp. gases.  Powder X-ray diffraction measurements indicate that the material is an extended three-dimensional analog of the well-known Hofmann clathrates which is formed through axial bridging of the in-plane octahedral Ni sites by the bidentate 1,2-bis(4-pyridyl)ethylene.  The hysteretic behavior toward guest adsorption and desorption is attributed to a structural phase transition in the material resulting from a variation in the tilt angle of the 1,2-bis(4-pyridyl)ethylene pillars.  Kinetics studies on the desorption of acetone from the material show two first-order processes with two rate consts. yielding activation energies of 68 and 55 kJ/mol when loadings are greater than 1 equiv of acetone per formula unit.  The CO2 adsorption/desorption isotherms on the series of structurally similar Ni(L)[Ni(CN)4] compds., where L = pyrazine, 4,4'-bipyridine, 1,2-bis(4-pyridyl)ethane, and dipyridylacetylene, are also reported.  In contrast to NiBpeneNiCN, the rigid members of this series show normal type I isotherms with no measureable hysteresis and no significant structural changes during the adsorption/desorption cycle, while the flexible 1,2-bis(4-pyridyl)ethane-bridged sample collapses in the guest-free state and shows no significant adsorption of CO2.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1039/c6ta04877c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Waste Biomass Derived Hard Carbon as a High-performance Anode Material for Sodium-ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A waste biomass derived hard carbon as a high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Pin; Li, Yunming; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Li, Hong; Chen, Liquan; Huang, Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
